### [2] SYSTEM PROMPT · 2026-07-03 18:25:58 UTC

```
<ai_inventor_context>
<ai_inventor_summary>
You are one of many LLMs in AI Inventor — an automated research system that generates NOVEL and FEASIBLE hypotheses, investigates them through experiments and research, and produces a paper.

Your output feeds other LLMs downstream. This demands your ABSOLUTE MAXIMUM reasoning — every output must be deeply thought out and maximally useful. Surface-level responses waste downstream computation.
</ai_inventor_summary>

<your_role>
YOU ARE: A research paper writer (Step 3.4: GEN_PAPER_TEXT in the invention loop)

You received the hypothesis, all artifacts, the previous paper draft (if any), and reviewer feedback.
Write a complete paper draft with figure placeholders.

Publication-quality paper → strong contribution. Weak paper → wasted iteration.
</your_role>
</ai_inventor_context>

<research_methodology>
Write like a researcher drafting a paper, not a chatbot summarizing bullet points.

- Structure as a paper would: research question → methodology → results → analysis → limitations. Not a list of "we did X, then Y."
- Ground every claim in specific artifacts and specific numbers. "Results show improvement" is empty — state effect sizes, baselines, and conditions.
- Be honest about what worked, what didn't, and why. Don't spin failures as "future work."
- The paper's headline contribution should be a positive or surprising finding. Negative results are valuable context but should not be the primary narrative — lead with what works.
- Address reviewer feedback from previous iterations explicitly — show you've thought about each critique.
</research_methodology>

<available_tools>
Web research is available through the aii-web-tools skill, in three levels (broad → specific):

1. web search — Returns titles, URLs, snippets. Use first to discover and scan the landscape.
2. web fetch — Reads a page and returns its content as markdown (HTML or PDF). Use to understand a source. May miss specific details — use fetch_grep below if it doesn't find what you need.
3. fetch_grep — Regex search over a page/PDF's full text. Returns exact matching sections with context. Use for precise details, exact numbers, methodology, or PDFs.

Workflow: search → fetch (understand) → fetch_grep (extract specifics).
</available_tools>

<tool_use>
Maximize parallel tool calls. Parallelize independent operations, only sequentialize dependencies.
- Multiple searches/fetches on different topics → parallel in one turn
- Search then fetch results → sequential (need URLs first)
</tool_use>

<system_reminder>
Do not ask follow up questions and do not ask the user anything. Execute all steps independently.
You must follow the todo list provided in each prompt exactly as written.
No placeholders, stubs, or incomplete code — all code must be complete and functional.
</system_reminder>

<process_isolation>
CRITICAL: Multiple pipeline runs may execute simultaneously on this machine. `ps aux | grep method.py` matches ALL runs, not just yours.
- NEVER kill processes by name (`killall`, `pkill -f`, `ps aux | grep ... | xargs kill`). This kills OTHER runs' processes.
- NEVER monitor processes by name (`ps aux | grep method.py`). You will see other runs' processes and get confused.
- ALWAYS use PID-based process management:
  Run: `uv run method.py & PID=$!` or `timeout <seconds> uv run method.py & PID=$!`
  Check: `kill -0 $PID 2>/dev/null && echo "Running" || echo "Ended"`
  Stop: `kill $PID`
  Wait: `wait $PID; echo "Exit code: $?"`
  Monitor: `tail -f logs/run.log & TAIL_PID=$!` then `kill $TAIL_PID` when done
</process_isolation>
```

### [3] SYSTEM-USER prompt · 2026-07-03 18:25:58 UTC

````
<available_domain_handbooks>
Domain handbooks below capture expert knowledge for a specific field — its landscape, prior work, dead ends, evaluation norms, and what counts as a genuinely novel contribution. If one is relevant to your research topic, READ that skill BEFORE proceeding; read the most relevant one(s), or none if none apply. Use it for related-work positioning and how this field frames a genuinely novel contribution.

- **aii-handbook-multi-llm-agents** — Guide for implementing Multi-LLM Agent Systems research using Mirascope orchestration, HuggingFace datasets/evaluation, and proven multi-agent patterns.
</available_domain_handbooks>
<hypothesis>
The research hypothesis.

kind: hypothesis
title: 'Rateless MinHash: Progressive Jaccard Estimation via Fountain Codes'
hypothesis: >-
  By viewing MinHash as a lossy compression channel and applying fountain code (rateless erasure code) principles, we can
  create a rateless MinHash sketch that generates a potentially infinite sequence of hash values, enabling progressive refinement
  of Jaccard similarity estimates. The optimal stopping point (sketch size) can be determined from rate-distortion theory,
  achieving optimal space-accuracy trade-offs without knowing the target similarity distribution beforehand.
motivation: >-
  MinHash and its variants require fixing the sketch size (number of hash functions) upfront, before knowing the desired estimation
  accuracy or the distribution of Jaccard similarities in the dataset. This leads to either wasted space (oversized sketches)
  or insufficient accuracy (undersized sketches). A rateless MinHash would solve this by generating hash values on-demand,
  allowing progressive refinement of estimates until a target accuracy is reached. This is particularly valuable for distributed
  deduplication, streaming data, and applications where the optimal sketch size is data-dependent.
assumptions:
- >-
  Jaccard similarity estimation can be framed as a rate-distortion problem where rate = sketch size and distortion = mean
  squared error in similarity estimation
- >-
  Fountain code principles (rateless coding) can be applied to generate a sequence of dependent hash values that together
  provide progressive Jaccard estimation
- >-
  The hash value sequence can be designed such that the estimation error decays monotonically as more values are received
- >-
  Rate-distortion theory can provide a computable bound for the optimal stopping point given a target estimation error
investigation_approach: >-
  1. Formalize MinHash as a rate-distortion problem: define the rate (sketch bits) and distortion (estimation error) for Jaccard
  similarity estimation. 2. Design a rateless MinHash scheme inspired by LT/Raptor fountain codes: generate a potentially
  infinite sequence of coded hash values from the set elements. 3. Derive the rate-distortion function for Jaccard estimation
  and use it to determine optimal stopping rules. 4. Implement the rateless MinHash algorithm and compare against standard
  MinHash, b-bit MinHash, and SetSketch on near-duplicate detection tasks. 5. Evaluate on LLM training data deduplication
  benchmarks (e.g., The Pile, C4) measuring space efficiency, estimation accuracy, and runtime.
success_criteria: >-
  The rateless MinHash scheme should: (1) Achieve the same Jaccard estimation accuracy as standard MinHash with less space
  on average (by adapting sketch size to each pair's similarity); (2) Provide progressive estimates that improve monotonically
  as more hash values are processed; (3) Have a computable stopping rule based on rate-distortion theory that achieves near-optimal
  space-accuracy trade-off; (4) Outperform fixed-size MinHash variants on datasets with diverse Jaccard similarity distributions.
related_works:
- >-
  Standard MinHash (Broder et al.) - uses k independent random permutations to estimate Jaccard similarity; our approach generalizes
  this to a rateless setting where k is not fixed upfront.
- >-
  b-bit MinHash (Li & Konig) - reduces sketch size by storing only b bits per hash value; our approach achieves variable sketch
  size through progressive refinement rather than fixed-size compression.
- >-
  SetSketch (Ertl) - bridges MinHash and HyperLogLog with a configurable parameter; our approach differs by making the sketch
  size itself adaptive rather than fixed at creation time.
- >-
  Polar Code Nearest-Neighbor (Touitou & Halabi) - uses error-correcting codes for LSH clustering; our approach applies coding
  theory to the hash function design itself, not just to the clustering step.
- >-
  ProbMinHash (Ertl) - computes probability Jaccard similarity for weighted sets; our approach addresses a different problem
  (rateless/progressive estimation) for unweighted Jaccard similarity.
- >-
  Odd Sketch (Mitzenmacher et al.) - uses binary sketches with XOR properties for high similarities; our approach targets
  the general case with progressive refinement rather than a fixed binary sketch.
inspiration: >-
  The hypothesis combines three cross-domain inspirations: (1) Fountain codes / rateless erasure codes from coding theory
  - where encoded symbols can be generated indefinitely and decoding becomes possible after receiving enough symbols; (2)
  Rate-distortion theory from information theory - which provides the optimal trade-off between compression rate and estimation
  distortion; (3) Progressive refinement from numerical analysis - where estimates are improved incrementally. The key insight
  is that MinHash sketches are essentially 'encoded' representations of sets, and by using coding-theoretic principles we
  can make this encoding rateless (adaptive to the desired accuracy).
terms:
- term: MinHash
  definition: >-
    A locality-sensitive hashing technique that estimates Jaccard similarity between two sets by computing the probability
    that the minimum hash value under a random permutation is the same for both sets.
- term: Jaccard similarity
  definition: >-
    A similarity measure for sets defined as the ratio of the intersection size to the union size: J(A,B) = |A∩B| / |A∪B|.
    It ranges from 0 (disjoint sets) to 1 (identical sets).
- term: Fountain code / rateless code
  definition: >-
    An erasure code that can generate an unlimited number of encoded symbols from a source message. The receiver can recover
    the original message after receiving any sufficient subset of encoded symbols, making the code 'rateless' (no fixed rate).
- term: Rate-distortion theory
  definition: >-
    A framework in information theory that studies the optimal trade-off between the compression rate (bits per symbol) and
    the resulting distortion (loss) in lossy data compression.
- term: Locality-sensitive hashing (LSH)
  definition: >-
    A family of hash functions where similar items are more likely to hash to the same value than dissimilar items. MinHash
    is an LSH family for Jaccard similarity.
- term: Progressive estimation
  definition: >-
    An estimation procedure where the accuracy improves monotonically as more computational resources (time, space, or data)
    are invested, without requiring the total resource budget to be fixed upfront.
summary: >-
  We propose Rateless MinHash, a new sketching approach that applies fountain code principles to MinHash, enabling progressive
  Jaccard similarity estimation where hash values are generated on-demand and the sketch size adapts to the desired accuracy.
  Rate-distortion theory provides the optimal stopping rule.
</hypothesis>

<all_artifacts>
FULL EVIDENCE BASE: All 3 research artifacts across all iterations.

--- Item 1 ---
id: art_9jz-SfB9wrwJ
type: research
title: Rateless MinHash Theory Foundations Research
summary: >-
  This research provides the theoretical foundations for Rateless MinHash, a novel sketching approach addressing the fundamental
  limitation of fixed sketch size in existing MinHash variants. Key findings include: (1) All current MinHash variants (Standard,
  b-bit, SetSketch, Odd Sketch, ProbMinHash) require upfront sketch size selection [1, 4, 6, 7, 8]. (2) Fountain codes (LT
  codes, Raptor codes) provide a proven rateless encoding framework where infinite symbol sequences can be generated with
  decoding possible from any slightly larger set [9, 10]. (3) The rate-distortion function for Jaccard similarity estimation
  remains undiscovered, though lower bounds exist from information complexity [3, 11, 12]. (4) A mathematical framework for
  Rateless MinHash is proposed, featuring encoding via degree-distributed hash generation, progressive decoding with decreasing
  variance O(1/k), and optimal stopping rules based on sequential estimation theory. The expected contribution is a sketch
  achieving near-optimal rate-distortion performance while adapting to diverse similarity regimes without upfront parameter
  tuning.
workspace_path: >-
  /ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1
out_expected_files:
- research_out.json

--- Item 2 ---
id: art_ZpaiuGemkOnz
type: dataset
title: Near-Duplicate Text Datasets for MinHash Evaluation
summary: >-
  This artifact provides 6 text datasets (Quora, 20 Newsgroups, MS MARCO, C4, AG News, synthetic) with 30K total documents
  and 3.5K duplicate pairs. The datasets include diverse text types from short questions to long documents, with controlled
  Jaccard similarity levels (0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9). Each document is tokenized and includes metadata with source dataset,
  duplicate ID, and similarity level. The data supports evaluation of Rateless MinHash and baseline methods on near-duplicate
  detection and Jaccard similarity estimation tasks. File size is 46MB, under the 100MB limit.
workspace_path: >-
  /ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_dataset_1

- data.py
- full_data_out.json
- preview_data_out.json
- mini_data_out.json

--- Item 3 ---
id: art_Q8_IBJsGhfEE
type: experiment
title: Progressive MinHash with Fountain Code Principles
summary: >-
  This experiment implements and evaluates a novel Rateless MinHash approach that uses fountain code principles (inspired
  by LT codes) to generate hash values progressively for adaptive Jaccard similarity estimation. The implementation includes
  StandardMinHash as baseline and RatelessMinHash as the novel method. Three experiments were conducted: (1) Error vs sketch
  size for standard MinHash showing expected MSE decrease with increasing k, (2) Progressive estimation with rateless MinHash
  achieving 55-80% improvement rate, (3) Space efficiency comparison showing adaptive rateless uses ~853 bits vs fixed 1024+
  bits. An additional equal-bits comparison reveals that rateless MinHash trades some statistical efficiency (1.01-1.93x higher
  error) for the flexibility of progressive estimation and adaptive stopping. The output includes method.py with complete
  implementation, method_out.json with experiment results validated against exp_gen_sol_out schema, visualization plots, and
  comprehensive documentation.
workspace_path: >-
  /ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_experiment_1
out_expected_files:
- method.py
- full_method_out.json
- mini_method_out.json
- preview_method_out.json
</all_artifacts>

{

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Technical Papers\n\nGuidance for the standard \"technical paper\" format: propose a method/system/framework, evaluate it experimentally, report results. This is the main track at most CS venues (NeurIPS, ICML, ICLR, ACL, AAAI, etc.). Does NOT cover: pure theory/formal proofs, survey papers, position papers, or dataset/benchmark papers \u2014 those have different structures.\n\n### Paper Structure\n\nTarget 6-8 pages. Use formal academic language, third person. Support claims with evidence from artifacts.\n\n#### Rough Page Budget (8-page paper)\n\n| Section | Pages | Notes |\n|---|---|---|\n| Abstract | 0.3 | Problem, approach, key result |\n| Introduction | 1.0-1.5 | The most important section |\n| Related Work | 0.5-1.0 | Beginning or end (see below) |\n| Methods | 1.5-2.0 | Architecture fig on page 1 |\n| Experiments | 1.5-2.0 | Setup + results + ablations |\n| Discussion | 0.5-1.0 | Limitations go here |\n| Conclusion | 0.3-0.5 | Do not repeat the abstract |\n| References | 0.5-1.0 | Not counted in page limit |\n\n**Critical rule**: A clear new technical contribution must be articulated by page 3 (quarter of the paper). If the reader doesn't know what you did by then, you've lost them.\n\n#### Section Details\n\n**Abstract** (150-250 words): State the problem, your approach, and the main results. Be factual and comprehensive. Do not repeat the abstract word-for-word later in the paper.\n\n**Introduction** \u2014 Follow this 5-paragraph structure:\n\n1. **What is the problem?** Define the task concretely.\n2. **Why is it interesting and important?** Real-world impact, scale.\n3. **Why is it hard?** Why do naive approaches fail?\n4. **Why hasn't it been solved before?** What's wrong with prior solutions? How does yours differ?\n5. **What are the key components of your approach and results?** Include specific limitations.\n\nEnd with a \"Summary of Contributions\" subsection \u2014 bullet list of contributions with section references. This doubles as an outline, saving space.\n\n**Related Work** \u2014 Placement decision:\n- **Beginning** (Section 2): If it can be short yet detailed, or if you need a strong defensive stance against prior work early.\n- **End** (before Conclusions): If comparisons require your technical content, or if it can be summarized briefly in the Introduction. Can be titled \"Discussion and Related Work.\"\n\n**Methods/Approach**: Every section tells a story \u2014 the story of the results, NOT the story of how you arrived at them. Use top-down description: readers should see where the material is going and be able to skip ahead. Move gory details to appendices.\n\n**Experiments**: Setup (datasets, metrics, baselines) \u2192 main results \u2192 ablations \u2192 analysis. Every claim needs quantitative evidence.\n\n**Discussion**: Interpret results, compare to prior work, state limitations honestly. Limitations should be specific and actionable, not vague disclaimers.\n\n**Conclusion**: Short summarizing paragraph. Do NOT repeat material from the Abstract or Introduction. Make original claims more concrete (e.g., reference quantitative results). Include future work as bullet list \u2014 if actively pursuing follow-up, say so to mark territory.\n\n#### Writing Quality Rules\n\n- Define all notation/terminology before use, only once. Group global definitions in Preliminaries.\n- Do NOT use nonreferential \"this\", \"that\", \"these\", \"it\". Always specify the referent. BAD: \"This is important because...\" GOOD: \"This accuracy gap is important because...\"\n- Do NOT use \"etc.\" unless remaining items are completely obvious. BAD: \"We measure volatility, scalability, etc.\" GOOD: \"We measure volatility and scalability.\"\n- Do NOT write \"for various reasons\" \u2014 state the actual reasons.\n- \"That\" is defining, \"which\" is nondefining. \"The algorithms that are easy to implement\" vs \"The algorithms, which are easy to implement.\"\n- Use italics for definitions and quotes, not for emphasis. Context alone should provide emphasis.\n\n### Figure Format\n\nFigures use a hybrid marker + structured array approach. ALL figures are generated by a separate pipeline step using an AI image model \u2014 your `image_gen_detailed_description` is the ONLY input that model sees. It cannot read files or access data. Do NOT generate actual image files yourself (no matplotlib, no PIL, no image generation scripts).\n\n**In paper_text**: Place `[FIGURE:fig_id]` markers where figures should appear.\n\n**In figures array**: Provide full specs as structured objects with these fields:\n- `id` \u2014 matches the `[FIGURE:id]` marker in paper_text\n- `title` \u2014 short descriptive title\n- `caption` \u2014 LaTeX caption that appears below the figure in the paper\n- `image_gen_detailed_description` \u2014 detailed prompt for the image generator (axes, ALL values, colors, layout)\n- `summary` \u2014 brief summary of what the figure communicates\n\nExample in paper_text:\n```\n...our method achieves state-of-the-art results as shown below.\n\n[FIGURE:fig_1]\n\nThe results in Figure 1 demonstrate...\n```\n\nExample figure spec in figures array:\n```json\n{\"id\": \"fig_1\", \"title\": \"Performance Comparison\", \"caption\": \"Comparison of geometric mean query latency across optimizers on JOB benchmark. RLQOpt achieves 2.3x speedup over PostgreSQL.\", \"image_gen_detailed_description\": \"Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: accuracy (0.0-1.0). Values: ModelA=0.847, ModelB=0.762, Baseline=0.531. Error bars with std: 0.02, 0.03, 0.05. Sans-serif font, white background.\", \"summary\": \"Compares accuracy of proposed methods vs baseline.\"}\n```\n\nEvery marker in text MUST have a matching figure in the array, and vice versa.\n\n#### Data Precision Requirement\n\n`image_gen_detailed_description` MUST include exact numbers from artifact output files. Read the actual output files before writing figure specs.\n\n- BAD: \"Compare accuracy metrics across configurations\"\n- GOOD: \"Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: accuracy (0.0-1.0). Values: K=3: 0.765, K=5: 0.729, Baseline: 0.121.\"\n\n#### Figure vs Table Decision\n\nDo NOT create figures for tabular data (rows/columns of text or numbers). Use `\\begin{table}` in LaTeX instead. Figures are for actual visualizations only (charts, plots, diagrams).\n\n#### Figure Placement Strategy\n\nBe intentional with figure ordering. The architectural/method overview figure explaining the proposed approach MUST appear early \u2014 in the Introduction or at the start of Methods \u2014 so readers can immediately orient themselves. Readers skim papers top-down; if the first figure they see is a results bar chart, they have no mental model for interpreting it.\n\nRecommended ordering:\n1. **Architecture/method diagram** \u2014 Introduction or early Methods (so readers understand the approach before diving into details)\n2. **Conceptual/analogy figures** \u2014 Introduction or Methods (to build intuition)\n3. **Results figures** (bar charts, line plots, scatter plots) \u2014 Results section\n4. **Analysis/ablation figures** \u2014 Discussion or later Results\n\n#### Guidelines\n\n- Plan 3-6 figures total across the paper\n- Place [FIGURE:fig_id] markers INLINE where referenced in text\n- Include axes, labels, ALL numeric values in figure descriptions\n- Both data-driven figures (bar charts, line plots) and conceptual diagrams (architecture, flowcharts)\n- Be as detailed as possible in descriptions: specify aspect ratio, preferred colors, all data values, axis labels, ranges, legend entries, and any other visual details. The more specific the description, the better the generated figure\n\n### Bibliography with Semantic Scholar\n\nBuild `./references.bib` using the aii-semscholar-bib skill (real BibTeX from Semantic Scholar):\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in one batch\n3. Write the returned .bib text into `./references.bib`\n\nRules:\n- Do NOT fabricate BibTeX entries \u2014 always fetch from Semantic Scholar\n- If a paper isn't found (very recent preprint), write the entry manually as fallback\n- Use `\\bibliography{references}` and `\\bibliographystyle{plainnat}`\n- Do NOT use inline `thebibliography` environment\n\n### Citation Format (for Research Artifacts)\n\nWhen writing research with numbered citations:\n\n1. Every factual claim MUST have a numbered citation: `[1]`, `[2]`, `[1, 3]`, etc.\n2. Each source in the \"sources\" array MUST have an \"index\" field\n3. The index MUST EXACTLY MATCH citation numbers in the text\n4. NEVER cite a number without a matching source index\n5. Example: \"LLMs show 40% improvement with multi-agent collaboration [1].\"\n\n---\nThis skill is located at `/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-paper-writing`. Any files it references (e.g. under `scripts/`, `references/`, `assets/`) are relative to that directory."}], "is_error": false, "skill_name": "aii-paper-writing", "kind": "InvokeSkillObservation"}

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Tool: `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch`\n\nBatch-fetch BibTeX entries from Semantic Scholar. Pass all references in a single call \u2014 the tool handles batching internally.\n\n### How it works\n\n1. **DOI/ArXiv refs** \u2192 batched into POST /paper/batch calls (up to 500 per API call, auto-chunked)\n2. **Title-only refs** \u2192 individual GET /paper/search/match (1s delay between)\n3. **Post-process** \u2192 fix entry type, fix citation key (AuthorYYYY), inject DOI\n\nThe ability server runs a single worker (`max_threads: 1`). Multiple concurrent tool calls are queued \u2014 each runs independently (no cross-request aggregation). Batching happens within each request.\n\n### Input format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"references\": [\n    {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n    {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n    {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nEach reference object can have:\n- `doi` \u2014 DOI string (ArXiv DOIs like `10.48550/arXiv.XXXX.XXXXX` auto-convert to ArXiv IDs)\n- `arxiv` \u2014 ArXiv ID (e.g. `\"2305.14325\"`)\n- `title` \u2014 Paper title (used for search/match when no DOI/ArXiv)\n- `author` \u2014 First author last name (for cleaner citation key)\n- `year` \u2014 Publication year (int, for citation key)\n\nAt least one of `doi`, `arxiv`, or `title` is required per reference.\n\n### Output format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"success\": true,\n  \"bib_text\": \"@inproceedings{Vaswani2017, ...}\\n\\n@article{Wei2022, ...}\",\n  \"total\": 3,\n  \"found\": 3,\n  \"failed_count\": 0,\n  \"entries\": [{\"citation_key\": \"Vaswani2017\", \"bibtex\": \"...\", \"title\": \"...\", \"doi\": \"...\", \"arxiv\": \"\"}],\n  \"failed\": []\n}\n```\n\n### Workflow\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in **one call**\n3. Save `bib_text` from the response to your `references.bib` file\n4. Check `failed` \u2014 for any missed papers, follow the **fallback procedure** below\n\n### Fallback for failed references (MANDATORY)\n\nNEVER fabricate BibTeX. For each failed reference:\n1. **WebSearch** for `\"Title\" author year` (try `site:arxiv.org` too)\n2. **WebFetch** the paper page \u2192 extract title, authors, year, venue, DOI/ArXiv ID\n3. If DOI/ArXiv found \u2192 retry `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with it\n4. Last resort: write BibTeX by hand using **only verified info from the actual paper page**\n\n---\n\n### CLI (for manual use / debugging)\n\n```bash\nSKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-semscholar-bib\" && \\\n$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python $SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_semscholar_bib__fetch.py --refs '[\n  {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n  {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n  {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n]'\n```\n\n`--json, -j` \u2014 output raw JSON instead of .bib text\n\n**If the script fails** with a connection error (ability server not running): create a local `.venv`, install server deps from `server_requirements.txt` into it, then import the `@aii_ability` function from the script and call it directly \u2014 bypassing the server:\n```bash\nuv venv .venv --python=3.12 && uv pip install --python=.venv/bin/python -r \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/server_requirements.txt\"\n```\n\n---\nThis skill is located at `/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-semscholar-bib`. Any files it references (e.g. under `scripts/`, `references/`, `assets/`) are relative to that directory."}], "is_error": false, "skill_name": "aii-semscholar-bib", "kind": "InvokeSkillObservation"}

The key findings are: (1) All current MinHash variants (Standard, b-bit, SetSketch, Odd Sketch, ProbMinHash) require upfront sketch size selection, preventing adaptive accuracy-space trade-offs [1, 4, 6, 7, 8]. (2) Fountain codes (LT codes, Raptor codes) provide a proven framework for rateless encoding where an infinite sequence of symbols can be generated, with decoding possible from any slightly larger set [9, 10]. (3) The rate-distortion function for Jaccard similarity estimation has not been derived, though lower bounds exist from information complexity [3, 11, 12]. (4) A mathematical framework for Rateless MinHash is proposed, including encoding via degree-distributed hash generation, progressive decoding with decreasing variance, and optimal stopping rules based on sequential estimation theory. The expected contribution is a sketch that can achieve near-optimal rate-distortion performance while adapting to diverse similarity regimes without upfront parameter tuning.\",\n     5\t  \"answer\": \"## Comprehensive Answer with Citations\\n\\n### 1. The Sketch Size Selection Problem in Existing MinHash Variants\\n\\nAll existing MinHash variants require the sketch size to be fixed upfront before seeing the data, which is a fundamental limitation:\\n\\n- **Standard MinHash** [1]: Uses k independent hash functions. The variance is Var[J\u0302] = J(1-J)/k, so to achieve expected error \u03b5 requires k = O(1/\u03b5\u00b2) [1]. The sketch size k must be chosen before knowing the actual Jaccard similarity J.\\n\\n- **b-bit MinHash** [4, 5]: Reduces storage by storing only the lowest b bits of each minhash. The variance becomes Var[J\u0302_b] = (1-J)/k \u00b7 (J + 1/(2^b - 1)) [5]. While more space-efficient, the compression is lossy and irreversible\u2014the sketch cannot be refined after creation [4].\\n\\n- **SetSketch** [6]: Has 4 parameters (m, b, a, q) that must be set before sketch construction [6, Section 2.3]. While it fills the gap between MinHash and HyperLogLog, it does not support progressive refinement.\\n\\n- **Odd Sketch** [7]: Optimized for high Jaccard similarities using a binary sketch. The sketch size n must be fixed upfront, and the estimator's accuracy depends on the fraction of 1s being bounded away from 1/2 [7, Section 2.2].\\n\\n- **ProbMinHash** [8]: Generates a finite signature of k hash values for weighted Jaccard similarity. The algorithm produces a fixed-size signature [8].\\n\\n**Synthesis**: The need to fix sketch size upfront is a universal limitation. If the actual Jaccard similarity differs from the expected range, the sketch either wastes space (if J is higher than expected) or provides insufficient accuracy (if J is lower than expected) [3].\\n\\n### 2. Fountain Code Principles Applicable to MinHash\\n\\nFountain codes provide a proven framework for rateless encoding that can be adapted to MinHash:\\n\\n- **LT Codes Encoding** [9]: Each encoding symbol is generated by (1) choosing degree d from distribution \u03c1(d), (2) selecting d distinct input symbols uniformly at random, (3) XORing these d symbols. The key property is that encoding symbols can be generated indefinitely\u2014the code is rateless [9, Section 1.1].\\n\\n- **Degree Distribution**: The Robust Soliton distribution ensures that with high probability, the decoder can recover all k input symbols from k + O(\u221a(k ln\u00b2(k/\u03b4))) encoding symbols [9]. This distribution carefully controls how many input symbols each encoding symbol combines.\\n\\n- **Decoding via Belief Propagation** [9, Section 2]: The decoder repeatedly finds encoding symbols of degree 1 (directly revealing one input symbol), XORs the recovered symbol into all neighbors to reduce their degree, and continues until all symbols are recovered.\\n\\n- **Raptor Codes** [10]: Improve upon LT codes with linear time encoding and decoding by applying a fixed-rate outer code before LT coding [10].\\n\\n**Analogy to MinHash**:\\n- In fountain codes, encoding symbols are random XOR combinations of input symbols.\\n- In MinHash, hash values are random selections from the set (the minimum under a random permutation).\\n- Both involve generating symbols from a set of inputs using a random process.\\n- The rateless property of fountain codes\u2014generating unlimited symbols and decoding from any slightly larger set\u2014can be translated to MinHash by generating an infinite sequence of hash values and estimating Jaccard similarity from progressively more values.\\n\\n**Research Gap**: No existing work applies fountain code principles to MinHash or similarity estimation. The conceptual connection is promising but unexplored.\\n\\n### 3. Rate-Distortion Theory for Jaccard Similarity Estimation\\n\\nRate-distortion theory provides the theoretical limits on lossy compression, which can bound the performance of Rateless MinHash:\\n\\n- **Rate-Distortion Function for Bernoulli Source** [11, 12]: For a Bernoulli(p) source with Hamming distortion (probability of error), the rate-distortion function is:\\n  R(D) = H_b(p) - H_b(D)  for 0 \u2264 D \u2264 min(p, 1-p)\\n  R(D) = 0                        for D > min(p, 1-p)\\n  where H_b(p) = -p log\u2082(p) - (1-p) log\u2082(1-p) is the binary entropy function [12].\\n\\n- **Connection to Jaccard Estimation**: If we model the presence/absence of each element in sets A and B as Bernoulli random variables, Jaccard similarity estimation resembles a distributed source coding problem. The rate R corresponds to the number of bits per element in the sketch, and the distortion D corresponds to the MSE of the Jaccard estimate.\\n\\n- **Lower Bound from Information Complexity** [3]: For estimating Jaccard similarity with relative error \u03b5J, any sketch must use \u03a9((1-J)/(\u03b5J)\u00b2) bits per set [3, Theorem 1.2]. This is derived by composing the Index problem (\u03a9(n) bits) and GapHamming problem (\u03a9(n) bits), yielding \u03a9(n/\u03b5\u00b2) total bits.\\n\\n- **Missing Derivation**: A thorough literature search revealed no existing work deriving the exact rate-distortion function R(D) for Jaccard similarity estimation. This is a critical gap that our work can fill.\\n\\n### 4. Progressive Estimation and Optimal Stopping Rules\\n\\nRateless MinHash enables progressive Jaccard similarity estimation, where accuracy improves as more hash values are processed. This requires a stopping rule:\\n\\n- **Sequential Estimation**: Using Hoeffding's inequality or Chernoff bounds, the number of hashes needed to achieve \u03b5 accuracy with probability 1-\u03b4 is k = O(J(1-J) log(1/\u03b4)/\u03b5\u00b2) [1].\\n\\n- **Sequential Probability Ratio Test (SPRT)** [13]: For hypothesis testing, SPRT provides an optimal stopping rule minimizing expected sample size while bounding error probabilities. This can be adapted to test whether |J\u0302 - J| < \u03b5.\\n\\n- **Confidence Interval-Based Stopping**: Compute a (1-\u03b1) confidence interval for J\u0302 after each hash value. Stop when the width of the confidence interval falls below a threshold 2\u03b5.\\n\\n- **Rate-Distortion-Informed Stopping**: Continue processing hash values until the total bits processed equals R(D), where D is the desired MSE. This provides a theoretically optimal stopping point if R(D) for Jaccard estimation is known.\\n\\n**Research Gap**: No existing MinHash implementation uses a sequential stopping rule.  5\tThis experiment implements and evaluates a novel \"Rateless MinHash\" approach that uses fountain code principles (inspired by LT codes) to generate hash values progressively for adaptive Jaccard similarity estimation.\n     6\t\n     7\t## Key Findings\n     8\t\n     9\t### Experiment 1: Error vs Sketch Size (Standard MinHash)\n    10\t- Standard MinHash shows expected behavior: MSE decreases as k increases\n    11\t- k=16: MSE = 0.0140\n    12\t- k=32: MSE = 0.0056\n    13\t- k=64: MSE = 0.0022\n    14\t- k=128: MSE = 0.0011\n    15\t\n    16\t### Experiment 2: Progressive Estimation (Rateless MinHash)\n    17\t- Rateless MinHash successfully enables progressive estimation\n    18\t- Error decreases as more coded hash values are processed (55-80% improvement rate)\n    19\t- Final MSE after 128 hashes ranges from 0.0017 to 0.0053 depending on true Jaccard\n    20\t\n    21\t### Experiment 3: Space Efficiency\n    22\t- Standard MinHash uses fixed bits: 512, 1024, 2048, or 4096 bits\n    23\t- Rateless MinHash with adaptive stopping uses ~853 bits on average (\u00b1148)\n    24\t- Adaptive approach can reduce space usage compared to using large fixed sketches\n    25\t\n    26\t### Equal-Bits Comparison\n    27\t- At equal bit budgets, standard MinHash still outperforms rateless MinHash\n    28\t- This is expected: standard MinHash uses independent hash functions, while rateless introduces dependencies via the coding process\n    29\t- Ratio of errors (Rateless/Standard): 1.01 to 1.93 depending on bit budget\n    30\t\n    31\t## Contributions\n    32\t\n    33\t1. **Novel Algorithm**: First implementation of rateless MinHash using fountain code principles\n    34\t2. **Progressive Estimation**: Demonstrated that Jaccard similarity can be estimated progressively\n    35\t3. **Adaptive Stopping**: Showed that estimation can stop when error is sufficiently low\n    36\t\n    37\t## Limitations\n    38\t\n    39\t1. **Dependent Hashes**: The coding process introduces dependencies that reduce statistical efficiency\n    40\t2. **Computational Overhead**: Generating coded hashes is more expensive than independent hashes\n    41\t3. **Theoretical Gap**: The degree distribution from LT codes may not be optimal for MinHash\n    42\t\n    43\t## Future Work\n    44\t\n    45\t1. Derive optimal degree distribution specifically for MinHash (not borrowed from LT codes)\n    46\t2. Explore other coding strategies (e.g., Raptor codes instead of LT codes)\n    47\t3. Test on real-world datasets (not just synthetic)\n    48\t4. Analyze theoretical variance bounds for rateless MinHash\n    49\t\n    50\t## Files\n    51\t\n    52\t- `method.py`: Complete implementation of StandardMinHash and RatelessMinHash\n    53\t- `method_out.json`: Detailed experiment results\n    54\t- `rateless_minhash_results.png`: Visualization of results\n    55\t- `logs/run.log`: Full experiment log\n    56\t\n"}], "is_error": false, "command": "view", "path": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_experiment_1/RESULTS.md", "prev_exist": true, "old_content": null, "new_content": null, "kind": "FileEditorObservation"}

````
{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Tool: `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch`\n\nBatch-fetch BibTeX entries from Semantic Scholar. Pass all references in a single call \u2014 the tool handles batching internally.\n\n### How it works\n\n1. **DOI/ArXiv refs** \u2192 batched into POST /paper/batch calls (up to 500 per API call, auto-chunked)\n2. **Title-only refs** \u2192 individual GET /paper/search/match (1s delay between)\n3. **Post-process** \u2192 fix entry type, fix citation key (AuthorYYYY), inject DOI\n\nThe ability server runs a single worker (`max_threads: 1`). Multiple concurrent tool calls are queued \u2014 each runs independently (no cross-request aggregation). Batching happens within each request.\n\n### Input format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"references\": [\n    {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n    {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n    {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nEach reference object can have:\n- `doi` \u2014 DOI string (ArXiv DOIs like `10.48550/arXiv.XXXX.XXXXX` auto-convert to ArXiv IDs)\n- `arxiv` \u2014 ArXiv ID (e.g. `\"2305.14325\"`)\n- `title` \u2014 Paper title (used for search/match when no DOI/ArXiv)\n- `author` \u2014 First author last name (for cleaner citation key)\n- `year` \u2014 Publication year (int, for citation key)\n\nAt least one of `doi`, `arxiv`, or `title` is required per reference.\n\n### Output format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"success\": true,\n  \"bib_text\": \"@inproceedings{Vaswani2017, ...}\\n\\n@article{Wei2022, ...}\",\n  \"total\": 3,\n  \"found\": 3,\n  \"failed_count\": 0,\n  \"entries\": [{\"citation_key\": \"Vaswani2017\", \"bibtex\": \"...\", \"title\": \"...\", \"doi\": \"...\", \"arxiv\": \"\"}],\n  \"failed\": []\n}\n```\n\n### Workflow\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in **one call**\n3. Save `bib_text` from the response to your `references.bib` file\n4. Check `failed` \u2014 for any missed papers, follow the **fallback procedure** below\n\n### Fallback for failed references (MANDATORY)\n\nNEVER fabricate BibTeX. For each failed reference:\n1. **WebSearch** for `\"Title\" author year` (try `site:arxiv.org` too)\n2. **WebFetch** the paper page \u2192 extract title, authors, year, venue, DOI/ArXiv ID\n3. If DOI/ArXiv found \u2192 retry `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with it\n4. Last resort: write BibTeX by hand using **only verified info from the actual paper page**\n\n---\n\n### CLI (for manual use / debugging)\n\n```bash\nSKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-semscholar-bib\" && \\\n$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python $SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_semscholar_bib__fetch.py --refs '[\n  {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n  {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n  {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n]'\n```\n\n`--json, -j` \u2014 output raw JSON instead of .bib text\n\n**If the script fails** with a connection error (ability server not running): create a local `.venv`, install server deps from `server_requirements.txt` into it, then import the `@aii_ability` function from the script and call it directly \u2014 bypassing the server:\n```bash\nuv venv .venv --python=3.12 && uv pip install --python=.venv/bin/python -r \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/server_requirements.txt\"\n```\n\n---\nThis skill is located at `/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-semscholar-bib`. Any files it references (e.g. under `scripts/`, `references/`, `assets/`) are relative to that directory."}], "is_error": false, "skill_name": "aii-semscholar-bib", "kind": "InvokeSkillObservation"}

This preserves the MinHash property that\n   129\t    the probability of hash match equals Jaccard similarity.\n   130\t    \"\"\"\n   131\t\n   132\t    def __init__(self, num_base_hashes: int = 64, seed: int = 42):\n   133\t        \"\"\"\n   134\t        Initialize RatelessMinHash.\n   135\t\n   136\t        Args:\n   137\t            num_base_hashes: Number of base hash functions (source symbols)\n   138\t            seed: Random seed for reproducibility\n   139\t        \"\"\"\n   140\t        self.num_base_hashes = num_base_hashes\n   141\t        self.base_seeds = [seed + i for i in range(num_base_hashes)]\n   142\t        self.rng = np.random.RandomState(seed)\n   143\t\n   144\t        # Use robust soliton for the degree distribution\n   145\t        self.degree_probs = self._robust_soliton(self.num_base_hashes)\n   146\t\n   147\t    def _robust_soliton(self, k: int) -> np.ndarray:\n   148\t        \"\"\"\n   149\t        Compute simplified Robust Soliton Distribution for LT codes.\n   150\t\n   151\t        Args:\n   152\t            k: Number of base hashes\n   153\t\n   154\t        Returns:\n   155\t            Probability distribution over degrees 1 to k\n   156\t        \"\"\"\n   157\t        c = 0.1\n   158\t        delta = 0.05\n   159\t        R = c * np.log(k / delta) * np.sqrt(k)\n   160\t\n   161\t        # Tau component (spike at end, decay toward beginning)\n   162\t        tau = np.zeros(k)\n   163\t        for d in range(1, k + 1):\n   164\t            if d < k / R:\n   165\t                tau[d-1] = R / (d * k)\n   166\t            elif d <= k / R:\n   167\t                tau[d-1] = R / (k * k / R)\n   168\t        tau[-1] += 1.0 / k  # Add spike at k\n   169\t\n   170\t        # Rho component (ideal soliton)\n   171\t        rho = np.zeros(k)\n   172\t        rho[0] = 1.0 / k\n   173\t        for d in range(2, k + 1):\n   174\t            rho[d-1] = 1.0 / (d * (d - 1))\n   175\t\n   176\t        # Combine and normalize\n   177\t        mu = tau + rho\n   178\t        mu = mu / np.sum(mu)\n   179\t        return mu\n   180\t\n   181\t    def _hash(self, elem: str, seed: int) -> float:\n   182\t        \"\"\"\n   183\t        Hash element with given seed, normalized to [0, 1].\n   184\t\n   185\t        Args:\n   186\t            elem: Element to hash\n   187\t            seed: Seed for hash function\n   188\t\n   189\t        Returns:\n   190\t            Normalized hash value in [0, 1]\n   191\t        \"\"\"\n   192\t        msg = f\"{seed}_{elem}\".encode()\n   193\t        h = hashlib.md5(msg).hexdigest()\n   194\t        return int(h[:8], 16) / 0xFFFFFFFF\n   195\t\n   196\t    def compute_base_hashes(self, elements: Set[str]) -> np.ndarray:\n   197\t        \"\"\"\n   198\t        Compute all base hash values for a set.\n   199\t\n   200\t        Args:\n   201\t            elements: Set of string elements\n   202\t\n   203\t        Returns:\n   204\t            Array of num_base_hashes minimum hash values\n   205\t        \"\"\"\n   206\t        base_hashes = np.full(self.num_base_hashes, np.inf)\n   207\t        for elem in elements:\n   208\t            for i, seed in enumerate(self.base_seeds):\n   209\t                h = self._hash(elem, seed)\n   210\t                base_hashes[i] = min(base_hashes[i], h)\n   211\t        return base_hashes\n   212\t\n   213\t    def generate_coded_hash_stream(self, base_hashes: np.ndarray,\n   214\t                                   indices_list: List[np.ndarray]) -> Iterator[float]:\n   215\t        \"\"\"\n   216\t        Generate coded hash stream using pre-sampled indices.\n   217\t\n   218\t        Key insight: For valid Jaccard estimation, the same indices must be\n   219\t        used for both sets. The coded hash is the MINIMUM of the selected\n   220\t        base hashes (not XOR). This preserves the MinHash property.\n   221\t\n   222\t        Args:\n   223\t            base_hashes: Base hash values for a set\n   224\t            indices_list: List of index arrays (same for both sets)\n   225\t\n   226\t        Yields:\n   227\t            Coded hash values (minimum of selected base hashes)\n   228\t        \"\"\"\n   229\t        for indices in indices_list:\n   230\t            # Take minimum of selected base hashes (MinHash property)\n   231\t            coded = np.min(base_hashes[indices])\n   232\t            yield coded\n   233\t\n   234\t    def generate_indices_stream(self, length: int, seed: int = None) -> List[np.ndarray]:\n   235\t        \"\"\"\n   236\t        Generate a stream of index arrays for coding.\n   237\t\n   238\t        Args:\n   239\t            length: Number of index arrays to generate\n   240\t            seed: Random seed (for reproducibility)\n   241\t\n   242\t        Returns:\n   243\t            List of numpy arrays containing indices to select\n   244\t        \"\"\"\n   245\t        rng = np.random.RandomState(seed) if seed is not None else self.rng\n   246\t        indices_list = []\n   247\t        for _ in range(length):\n   248\t            # Sample degree from distribution\n   249\t            d = rng.choice(range(1, self.num_base_hashes + 1), p=self.degree_probs)\n   250\t            # Select d base hashes uniformly at random\n"}], "is_error": false, "command": "view", "path": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_sAQsTTaaqjOV/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_experiment_1/method.py", "prev_exist": true, "old_content": null, "new_content": null, "kind": "FileEditorObservation"}

The key findings are: (1) All current MinHash variants (Standard, b-bit, SetSketch, Odd Sketch, ProbMinHash) require upfront sketch size selection, preventing adaptive accuracy-space trade-offs [1, 4, 6, 7, 8]. (2) Fountain codes (LT codes, Raptor codes) provide a proven framework for rateless encoding where an infinite sequence of symbols can be generated, with decoding possible from any slightly larger set [9, 10]. (3) The rate-distortion function for Jaccard similarity estimation has not been derived, though lower bounds exist from information complexity [3, 11, 12]. The expected contribution is a sketch that can achieve near-optimal rate-distortion performance while adapting to diverse similarity regimes without upfront parameter tuning.\",\n     5\t  \"answer\": \"## Comprehensive Answer with Citations\\n\\n### 1. The Sketch Size Selection Problem in Existing MinHash Variants\\n\\nAll existing MinHash variants require the sketch size to be fixed upfront before seeing the data, which is a fundamental limitation:\\n\\n- **Standard MinHash** [1]: Uses k independent hash functions. The variance is Var[J\u0302] = J(1-J)/k, so to achieve expected error \u03b5 requires k = O(1/\u03b5\u00b2) [1]. The sketch size k must be chosen before knowing the actual Jaccard similarity J.\\n\\n- **b-bit MinHash** [4, 5]: Reduces storage by storing only the lowest b bits of each minhash. The variance becomes Var[J\u0302_b] = (1-J)/k \u00b7 (J + 1/(2^b - 1)) [5]. While more space-efficient, the compression is lossy and irreversible\u2014the sketch cannot be refined after creation [4].\\n\\n- **SetSketch** [6]: Has 4 parameters (m, b, a, q) that must be set before sketch construction [6, Section 2.3]. While it fills the gap between MinHash and HyperLogLog, it does not support progressive refinement.\\n\\n- **Odd Sketch** [7]: Optimized for high Jaccard similarities using a binary sketch. The sketch size n must be fixed upfront, and the estimator's accuracy depends on the fraction of 1s being bounded away from 1/2 [7, Section 2.2].\\n\\n- **ProbMinHash** [8]: Generates a finite signature of k hash values for weighted Jaccard similarity. The algorithm produces a fixed-size signature [8].\\n\\n**Synthesis**: The need to fix sketch size upfront is a universal limitation. If the actual Jaccard similarity differs from the expected range, the sketch either wastes space (if J is higher than expected) or provides insufficient accuracy (if J is lower than expected) [3].\\n\\n### 2. Fountain Code Principles Applicable to MinHash\\n\\nFountain codes provide a proven framework for rateless encoding that can be adapted to MinHash:\\n\\n- **LT Codes Encoding** [9]: Each encoding symbol is generated by (1) choosing degree d from distribution \u03c1(d), (2) selecting d distinct input symbols uniformly at random, (3) XORing these d symbols. The key property is that encoding symbols can be generated indefinitely\u2014the code is rateless [9, Section 1.1].\\n\\n- **Degree Distribution**: The Robust Soliton distribution ensures that with high probability, the decoder can recover all k input symbols from k + O(\u221a(k ln\u00b2(k/\u03b4))) encoding symbols [9]. This distribution carefully controls how many input symbols each encoding symbol combines.\\n\\n- **Decoding via Belief Propagation** [9, Section 2]: The decoder repeatedly finds encoding symbols of degree 1 (directly revealing one input symbol), XORs the recovered symbol into all neighbors to reduce their degree, and continues until all symbols are recovered.\\n\\n- **Raptor Codes** [10]: Improve upon LT codes with linear time encoding and decoding by applying a fixed-rate outer code before LT coding [10].\\n\\n**Analogy to MinHash**:\\n- In fountain codes, encoding symbols are random XOR combinations of input symbols.\\n- In MinHash, hash values are random selections from the set (the minimum under a random permutation).\\n- Both involve generating symbols from a set of inputs using a random process.\\n- The rateless property of fountain codes\u2014generating unlimited symbols and decoding from any slightly larger set\u2014can be translated to MinHash by generating an infinite sequence of hash values and estimating Jaccard similarity from progressively more values.\\n\\n**Research Gap**: No existing work applies fountain code principles to MinHash or similarity estimation. The conceptual connection is promising but unexplored.\\n\\n### 3. Rate-Distortion Theory for Jaccard Similarity Estimation\\n\\nRate-distortion theory provides the theoretical limits on lossy compression, which can bound the performance of Rateless MinHash:\\n\\n- **Rate-Distortion Function for Bernoulli Source** [11, 12]: For a Bernoulli(p) source with Hamming distortion (probability of error), the rate-distortion function is:\\n  R(D) = H_b(p) - H_b(D)  for 0 \u2264 D \u2264 min(p, 1-p)\\n  R(D) = 0                        for D > min(p, 1-p)\\n  where H_b(p) = -p log\u2082(p) - (1-p) log\u2082(1-p) is the binary entropy function [12].\\n\\n- **Connection to Jaccard Estimation**: If we model the presence/absence of each element in sets A and B as Bernoulli random variables, Jaccard similarity estimation resembles a distributed source coding problem. The rate R corresponds to the number of bits per element in the sketch, and the distortion D corresponds to the MSE of the Jaccard estimate.\\n\\n- **Lower Bound from Information Complexity** [3]: For estimating Jaccard similarity with relative error \u03b5J, any sketch must use \u03a9((1-J)/(\u03b5J)\u00b2) bits per set [3, Theorem 1.2]. This is derived by composing the Index problem (\u03a9(n) bits) and GapHamming problem (\u03a9(n) bits), yielding \u03a9(n/\u03b5\u00b2) total bits.\\n\\n- **Missing Derivation**: A thorough literature search revealed no existing work deriving the exact rate-distortion function R(D) for Jaccard similarity estimation. This is a critical gap that our work can fill.\\n\\n### 4. Progressive Estimation and Optimal Stopping Rules\\n\\nRateless MinHash enables progressive Jaccard similarity estimation, where accuracy improves as more hash values are processed. This requires a stopping rule:\\n\\n- **Sequential Estimation**: Using Hoeffding's inequality or Chernoff bounds, the number of hashes needed to achieve \u03b5 accuracy with probability 1-\u03b4 is k = O(J(1-J) log(1/\u03b4)/\u03b5\u00b2) [1].\\n\\n- **Sequential Probability Ratio Test (SPRT)** [13]: For hypothesis testing, SPRT provides an optimal stopping rule minimizing expected sample size while bounding error probabilities. This can be adapted to test whether |J\u0302 - J| < \u03b5.\\n\\n- **Confidence Interval-Based Stopping**: Compute a (1-\u03b1) confidence interval for J\u0302 after each hash value. Stop when the width of the confidence interval falls below a threshold 2\u03b5.\\n\\n- **Rate-Distortion-Informed Stopping**: Continue processing hash values until the total bits processed equals R(D), where D is the desired MSE. This provides a theoretically optimal stopping point if R(D) for Jaccard estimation is known.\\n\\n**Research Gap**: No existing MinHash implementation uses a sequential stopping rule. []\n   269\t        matches = 0\n   270\t\n   271\t        min_len = min(len(stream1), len(stream2))\n   272\t        for i in range(min_len):\n   273\t            if abs(stream1[i] - stream2[i]) < 1e-10:  # Float comparison\n   274\t                matches += 1\n   275\t            estimates.append(matches / (i + 1))\n   276\t            num_processed.append(i + 1)\n   277\t\n   278\t        return np.array(estimates), np.array(num_processed)\n   279\t\n   280\t    def generate_stream_fixed_length(self, elements: Set[str], length: int,\n   281\t                                     seed: int = None) -> Tuple[List[float], List[np.ndarray]]:\n   282\t        \"\"\"\n   283\t        Generate a fixed-length coded hash stream for a set.\n   284\t\n   285\t        Args:\n   286\t            elements: Set of elements\n   287\t            length: Desired stream length\n   288\t            seed: Random seed for index generation\n   289\t\n   290\t        Returns:\n   291\t            Tuple of (hash stream, indices list)\n   292\t        \"\"\"\n   293\t        base_hashes = self.compute_base_hashes(elements)\n   294\t        indices_list = self.generate_indices_stream(length, seed)\n   295\t        hash_stream = list(self.generate_coded_hash_stream(base_hashes, indices_list))\n   296\t        return hash_stream, indices_list\n   297\t\n   298\t\n   299\tdef generate_synthetic_sets(\n   300\t    num_pairs: int,\n   301\t    jaccard_targets: List[float],\n   302\t    set_size: int = 100,\n   303\t    vocab_size: int = 1000\n   304\t) -> List[Tuple[Set[str], Set[str], float]]:\n   305\t    \"\"\"\n   306\t    Generate synthetic set pairs with controlled Jaccard similarity.\n   307\t\n   308\t    Args:\n   309\t        num_pairs: Total number of pairs to generate\n   310\t        jaccard_targets: List of target Jaccard values\n   311\t        set_size: Size of each set\n   312\t        vocab_size: Size of vocabulary to draw elements from\n   313\t\n   314\t    Returns:\n   315\t        List of (set1, set2, true_jaccard) tuples\n   316\t    \"\"\"\n   317\t    all_elements = [f\"elem_{i}\" for i in range(vocab_size)]\n   318\t\n   319\t    pairs = []\n   320\t    pairs_per_target = num_pairs // len(jaccard_targets)\n   321\t\n   322\t    for target_j in jaccard_targets:\n   323\t        for _ in range(pairs_per_target):\n   324\t            # Generate sets with target Jaccard\n   325\t            # J(A,B) = |A\u2229B| / |A\u222aB|\n   326\t            # Let |A| = |B| = n, |A\u2229B| = m\n   327\t            # Then J = m / (2n - m) => m = J * 2n / (1 + J)\n   328\t            n = set_size\n   329\t            m = max(1, int(target_j * 2 * n / (1 + target_j)))\n   330\t\n   331\t            # Ensure m <= n (otherwise intersection larger than set)\n   332\t            m = min(m, n)\n   333\t\n   334\t            all_indices = np.random.permutation(vocab_size)\n   335\t            intersection = set(all_indices[:m])\n   336\t            remaining = all_indices[m:]\n   337\t\n   338\t            # Split remaining indices between set_a and set_b\n   339\t            rest_a_size = min(n - m, len(remaining) // 2)\n   340\t            rest_b_size = min(n - m, len(remaining) - rest_a_size)\n   341\t\n   342\t            rest_a = set(remaining[:rest_a_size])\n   343\t            rest_b = set(remaining[rest_a_size:rest_a_size + rest_b_size])\n   344\t\n   345\t            set_a = intersection | rest_a\n   346\t            set_b = intersection | rest_b\n   347\t\n   348\t            # Verify Jaccard\n   349\t            true_j = len(set_a & set_b) / len(set_a | set_b)\n   350\t\n   351\t            pairs.append((\n   352\t                {f\"elem_{i}\" for i in set_a},\n   353\t                {f\"elem_{i}\" for i in set_b},\n   354\t                true_j\n   355\t            ))\n   356\t\n   357\t    return pairs\n   358\t\n   359\t\n   360\tdef run_experiment_1_error_vs_sketch(\n   361\t    pairs: List[Tuple[Set[str], Set[str], float]],\n   362\t    k_values: List[int]\n   363\t) -> Dict[int, float]:\n   364\t    \"\"\"\n   365\t    Experiment 1: Compare MSE of standard MinHash for different sketch sizes.\n   366\t\n   367\t    Args:\n   368\t        pairs: List of (set1, set2, true_jaccard) tuples\n   369\t        k_values: List of sketch sizes to test\n   370\t\n   371\t    Returns:\n   372\t        Dictionary mapping k to average MSE\n   373\t    \"\"\"\n   374\t    logger.info(\"=== Experiment 1: Error vs Sketch Size ===\")\n   375\t\n   376\t    results = {}\n   377\t\n   378\t    for k in k_values:\n   379\t        std_minhash = StandardMinHash(k=k)\n   380\t        errors = []\n   381\t\n   382\t        for set_a, set_b, true_j in pairs:\n   383\t            sig_a = std_minhash.compute_signature(set_a)\n   384\t            sig_b = std_minhash.compute_signature(set_b)\n   385\t            est_j = std_minhash.estimate_jaccard(sig_a, sig_b)\n   386\t            errors.append((est_j - true_j) ** 2)  # MSE\n   387\t\n   388\t        avg_mse = np.mean(errors)\n   389\t        std_mse = np.std(errors)\n   390\t        results[k] = {'mse': avg_mse, 'std': std_mse}\n   391\t        logger.info(f\"Standard MinHash k={k}: MSE = {avg_mse:.6f} \u00b1 {std_mse:.6f}\")\n   392\t\n   393\t    return results\n   394\t\n   395\t\n   396\tdef run_experiment_2_progressive_estimation(\n   397\t    pairs: List[Tuple[Set[str], Set[str], float]],\n   398\t    jaccard_targets: List[float],\n   399\t    max_stream_len: int = 128,\n   400\t    num_base_hashes: int = 128\n"}], "is_error": false, "command": "view", "path":
